      <div class="vn-textwrap">
       <h1 class="vn-title">
        The Order: 1886 Looks Really Cool When You Shoot Grenades in Midair; Info on Length &amp; Replayability
       </h1>
       <div class="KonaBody">
        <p class="vn-text">
         Once upon a time, Ready at Dawn's Andrea Pessino mentioned that by using Black Sight it's posssible to shoot grenades in midair in The Order: 1886, and today we get to see it in action, on top of some info on length and replayability.
         <span class="vn-tags">
          (
          <a class="" href="/channel/ps4" title="">
           PS4
          </a>
          ,
          <a class="" href="/channel/the-order-1886" title="PS4">
           The Order: 1886
          </a>
          )
         </span>
        </p>
       </div>
       <div style="float: right;">
       </div>

            </a>
            <a class="cl-umb" href="/Comments/GetComment?commentId=10139426&amp;mode=9&amp;state=1" onclick="Sys.Mvc.AsyncHyperlink.handleClick(this, new Sys.UI.DomEvent(event), { insertionMode: Sys.Mvc.InsertionMode.replace, updateTargetId: 'c-10139426' });">
             +
            </a>
            153d ago
           </div>
           <div class="cl-con">
            @Pedro93
            <br/>
            Not necessarily in my opinion, the timing of reviews has more to do with politics and behind the scenes decisions. I actually like it when the review comes out AFTER a game releases. There is a battle taking place behind the scenes between developers and journalists. Traditional gaming journalism has reached a point where the quality of a game is only one aspect of what gets a good review. Often times, the developer's decision to give exclusive "review rights" to certain websites (who knows maybe even money exchanges hands), is at least ONE of the factors that leads to a good game review score. Unfortunately, this type of business has been going on for a while now. It has led to the fattening of the pockets of the bigger gaming journalism websites (we all know who they are) and the rest of the journalists tend to follow the lead of what these bigger sites decide a game deserves. Other smaller journalist/websites rely on "clickbait" journalism because they are not inside the circle of sites that get paid to review well. So they rely on other means to make money. Some developers are tired of this trend, especially those who are independent. They don't want to spend resources that could go into their investment, in order to get a good review. They opt not to give the "right to review" to any journalists until AFTER the game released, at which point the journalist websites would not have time to damage the sales of the game too much because the consumers have already begun purchasing the game, and also because the reviewer has less room to give the game a crappy review score lest they get singled out because the game can speak for itself.
            <br/>
            So, personally i don't think the timing of review scores is relevant. You might have a good game that gets reviewed after release, and vise versa. But this is my personal opinion, so take it with a grain of salt.
           </div>
